<div id="review_3317521509" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3317521509" />
    <a title="Chavelli Sulikowska" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16127160-chavelli-sulikowska"><img alt="Chavelli Sulikowska" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534643154p2/16127160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3317521509?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chavelli Sulikowska" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chavelli Sulikowska" href="/user/show/16127160-chavelli-sulikowska">Chavelli Sulikowska</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781793180261" href="/book/show/43486510-white-nights">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16127160-chavelli-sulikowska?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3317521509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4247781373254354783">“I don’t know how to be silent when my heart is speaking.” The Ruskie classicists really do self indulge in the painful pleasure of unrequited love. I also read Turgenev’s First Love earlier this year and found a similar, poor, desperate young hero who falls victim to a woman’s indifference.<br/><br/>Reminiscent, reflective and romantic is how I would describe this apparently underappreciated novella…“I like revisiting, at certain times, spots where I was once happy; I like to shape the present in the ima</span>
  <span id="freeText4247781373254354783" style="display:none">“I don’t know how to be silent when my heart is speaking.” The Ruskie classicists really do self indulge in the painful pleasure of unrequited love. I also read Turgenev’s First Love earlier this year and found a similar, poor, desperate young hero who falls victim to a woman’s indifference.<br /><br />Reminiscent, reflective and romantic is how I would describe this apparently underappreciated novella…“I like revisiting, at certain times, spots where I was once happy; I like to shape the present in the image of the irretrievable past.”<br /><br />The unnamed narrator is a lonely dreamer, he is both hopeful but hopeless! As a reader, we feel sympathy for his sorrow, but also frustration at his naivety. He seems a glutton for punishment, just asking to be taken for a ride or beaten over the head again and again! There are equal moments of tenderness and anguish apparent in this novella – “you would never feel that my love was a burden to you; you would only feel every minute that at your side is a beating grateful heart…” <br /><br />I much preferred Turgenev’s novella. However, Dostoyevsky does write with supreme elegance and descriptive licence “either the sunbeams suddenly peeping out from the clouds for a moment were hidden again behing a veil of rain and everything had grown ugly again before my eyes, or perhaps the whole vista of my future flashed before me, so sad, so forbidding…” <br /><br />White Nights demonstrates Dostoyevskys’s exemplary writing, often characterised as existentialist, I found the story mundane and the characters a little flat – one dimensional you could say. I am sure there are superior examples of Dostoyevsky’s talent. However, despite not enjoying the story or the characters, I did relish his little pearls of insight and wisdom nestled throughout – “when we are unhappy, we feel the unhappiness of others more; feeling is not destroyed but concentrated…” if you are a perceptive and empathetic person, yes, how true this is…<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4247781373254354783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3317521509">

I think, this is why I am very much attracted to this one.<br /><br /><br />"I have lived, as they say, keeping myself to myself, that is, utterly alone-alone entirely alone. Do you know what it means to be alone?"<br /><br />"They, of course, do not know me, but I know them. I know them intimately, I have almost made a study of their faces, and am delighted when they are gay, and downcast when they are under a cloud."<br /><br />"to be too conscious is an illness—a real thorough-going illness.” <br /><br />“I don’t know how to be silent when my heart is speaking.” <br /><br />“But how could you live and have no story to tell?” <br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1720687940" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1720687940" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720687940?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bestest">bestest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=7-russian">7-russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=sad-lonely-thinking-of-suicide">sad-lonely-thinking-of-suicide</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720687940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10354290637786448694"><blockquote>
  <i>“Why is it that even the best of men always seem to hide something from other people and to keep something back? Why not say straight out what is in one's heart, when one knows that one is not speaking idly? As it is everyone seems harsher than he really is, as though all were afraid of doing injustice to their feelings, by being too quick to express them."<br/></i>
</blockquote><br/>It is not so in FD’s works. In FD’s world, everybody is talking about his feelings. Even the most quiet and loneliest of people - one of </span>
  <span id="freeText10354290637786448694" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“Why is it that even the best of men always seem to hide something from other people and to keep something back? Why not say straight out what is in one's heart, when one knows that one is not speaking idly? As it is everyone seems harsher than he really is, as though all were afraid of doing injustice to their feelings, by being too quick to express them."<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />It is not so in FD’s works. In FD’s world, everybody is talking about his feelings. Even the most quiet and loneliest of people - one of things that set him apart. This time it is guy who hasn’t talked to anyone for days. The reason – well, he likes talking about his feelings. <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“ I don't know how to be silent when my heart is speaking.” <br /></i>
</blockquote><br />Loneliness yields to fantasies – and soon he becomes a dreamer. He dreams of whole love stories he has lived through. But it is not like other dreams, while he knows he is dreaming – that doesn’t keep him from getting emotionally invested in them. Like Pygmalion, he falls in love with women he dreamed up.<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“And I do nothing but dream every day that at last I shall meet some one. Oh, if only you knew how often I have been in love in that way...."<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />Such dreams are pure vice – they are addictive and they charge a heavy long term price for temporary relief. And once you come out of your dreamed worlds, reality seem a lot more harsher.<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“dreamer—if you want an exact definition—is not a human being, but a creature of an intermediate sort. For the most part he settles in some inaccessible corner, as though hiding from the light of day; once he slips into his corner, he grows to it like a snail, or, anyway, he is in that respect very much like that remarkable creature, which is an animal and a house both at once, and is called a tortoise.”<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />You remember that ‘Mirror of Erised’ – dreams and fancies are like that. The mirror was to show our ‘our deepest, most desperate dreams of our heart.’ (‘Erised’ is ‘desire’ spelled backwards). Dumbledore had warned Harry on how people wasted their whole lives on that. Our story-teller was probably one of them.<br /><br />D sometimes tests one’s patience. No one should be allowed to be that accurate. Anyway, the story is about how this tortoise meets a girl who too has been forced to live alone. As you can expect, there is a love story – their dialogue is not much unlike lyrics of song ‘way back into love’ (from ‘Music and Lyrics’) -the ‘shadow over head’ and all; but if you know D at all then I don’t think I will be spoilish to exclaim <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ how come none of D’s heroes ever gets the girl! And though narrator might have been happy enough with the memory he gained when he wrote this account, I think in long run – it too, like his dreams, shall become a cause of suffering for him. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="10354290637786448694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2083018153" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2083018153" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2083018153?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=russia-ukraine">russia-ukraine</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2083018153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1253981518746750482">The unnamed narrator of White Nights, as he goes about telling us himself, is a bit of a dreamer, a loner, with no real friends to call friends. He only knows the look and soul of the physical places around his city, Saint Petersburg. Hiding from the day, he wanders aimlessly around the city at night, animating each street corner, trying to fill the voids of his existence. Dostoyevsky’s short novel lasts for four nights. On the first night, the dreamer meets a crying woman, and bravely approache</span>
  <span id="freeText1253981518746750482" style="display:none">The unnamed narrator of White Nights, as he goes about telling us himself, is a bit of a dreamer, a loner, with no real friends to call friends. He only knows the look and soul of the physical places around his city, Saint Petersburg. Hiding from the day, he wanders aimlessly around the city at night, animating each street corner, trying to fill the voids of his existence. Dostoyevsky’s short novel lasts for four nights. On the first night, the dreamer meets a crying woman, and bravely approaches her as he’s never approached anyone before, and consoles her. Although she is engaged to another man, they end up spending four nights getting to know each other. The closer he feels to her, the more he distances himself from his lonely life. Has he finally found the one chance he’ll ever have to rise above the city from which he feels estranged. The two protagonists approach an unbearable crescendo until the final moment, when Dostoyevsky ends his story as abruptly as it had begun, and the man suddenly sees even the physical city as a lifeless and meaningless place.<br /><br />This is one of those timeless, poignant, simple stories that shows us again Dostoyevsky was one of the true great writers. I guess you call this a sort of sad love story, but it's one that's refreshingly different. It depicts a person who is truly in love with another person, and not with the idea of love, or with some kind of fantastical vision of what their life could be like together. Somehow, it has the power to leave you scarred, but also hopeful. Dostoyevsky's emotional intense prose really is quite something, and although nowhere near as deep as his epics, I think it's one of my faves, and one to read again and again, hopefully. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1253981518746750482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5359485068184088382" style="display:none">"I have lived, as they say, keeping myself to myself, that is, utterly alone-alone entirely alone. Do you know what it means to be alone?"<br /><br />I think Dostoyevsky knows the answer very well, again i thought and asked to myself what kind of an extraordinary life that this man lived inspiring him to create extraordinary characters like the one in this short story. <br /><br />I wish i could learn the name of the hero of the story. <div id="review_1889158025" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1889158025" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1889158025?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863523-laysee?shelf=five-star-books">five-star-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1889158025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11454769247945586372"><br/>Night is that time of day when loneliness tends to be amplified. “White Nights” is set in summer time in St Petersburg but it has none of the warmth and cheeriness one associates with summer. The narrator has no name and this could well be the story of anyone who finds himself in the same predicament. “White Nights” is subtitled “a sentimental story from the diary of a dreamer”. It is a very short, sad, love story but it packs in psychological insights that are real and true. <br/>	<br/>It is the holiday </span>
  <span id="freeText11454769247945586372" style="display:none"><br />Night is that time of day when loneliness tends to be amplified. “White Nights” is set in summer time in St Petersburg but it has none of the warmth and cheeriness one associates with summer. The narrator has no name and this could well be the story of anyone who finds himself in the same predicament. “White Nights” is subtitled “a sentimental story from the diary of a dreamer”. It is a very short, sad, love story but it packs in psychological insights that are real and true. <br />	<br />It is the holiday season and people have left for their summer villas. The narrator who professed to have no friends feels lonelier than ever. He makes friends of houses and has conversations with them in his mind. He is a dreamer who claims to be often in love. But when asked with whom he is in love, his reply is, "Why, with no one, with an ideal, with the one I dream of in my sleep." I can hear alarm bells ringing as I read.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[On one of his solitary walks, he comes to the rescue of a young lady (Nastenka) whom he sees weeping, and they become acquainted. His manner is so unnervingly importunate that a woman can easily be put off. In fact, his outpouring of his tortured soul to a stranger is worrying and suggests a rather poor awareness of social boundaries. They have a few dates and it becomes clear that temperamentally, he cannot be more different from Nastenka. He is all dark clouds; she is the bright sun. It is a relationship that does not bode an optimistic future. Yet, Dostoyevsky captures beautifully the rapture of their nightly meetings. "Oh, the coquetry of happiness!" The initial felicity and hopefulness of an early friendship or courtship is palpable and sympathetically conveyed. Never mind the fragility of the connection that is evident right from the beginning. <br /><br />As it turns out, quite predictably, the man's love is unrequited. And one must have a heart of stone not to feel the enormity of his loss and grief. Dostoyevsky's sensitive telling of the throes of despair in the experience of love lost touched me deeply. It also shifted my perception of the lovelorn man. At the end of the story, I was left not with a sense of his strangeness but with gratitude for his magnanimity toward the girl he loved. To rejoice in the happiness of a loved one for whom one's heart bleeds for reciprocation is a humanly difficult but divinely loving response.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I am reading Dostoyevsky for the first time and it will not be the last. Five tear-stained stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11454769247945586372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HnwD9e0r4IBZVeM8YHJUqUBIWC91kn0ydUO1X+tP5LEkWmonVnUp6P5+V12Xh152lhe/57sf81UuPfPuJgwWKw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XtLx5rWdrgth2IgOqNVXXoE+9s/V5oDvGgqIg+Cpb2Vk9Jg0DsNnY8bzPG9fIF2BV2ERBxtrDohBdM4yLeqd/w==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Zweig okurunu, bir kez daha, insan psikolojisinde eşine pek rastlanmayan bir yolculuğa davet ediyor.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky">About Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1506003555p3/3137322.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8440117379729393047">Fyodor Mikhailovich Dostoevsky was born in Moscow in 1821. His debut, the epistolary novella Poor Folk (1846), made his name. In 1849 he was arrested for involvement with the politically subversive 'Petrashevsky circle' and until 1854 he lived in a convict prison in Omsk, Siberia. From this experience came The House of the Dead (1860-2). In 1860 he began the journal Vremya (Time). Already married,</span>
  <span id="freeText8440117379729393047" style="display:none">Fyodor Mikhailovich Dostoevsky was born in Moscow in 1821. His debut, the epistolary novella Poor Folk (1846), made his name. In 1849 he was arrested for involvement with the politically subversive 'Petrashevsky circle' and until 1854 he lived in a convict prison in Omsk, Siberia. From this experience came The House of the Dead (1860-2). In 1860 he began the journal Vremya (Time). Already married, he fell in love with one of his contributors, Appollinaria Suslova, eighteen years his junior, and developed a ruinous passion for roulette. After the death of his first wife, Maria, in 1864, Dostoyevsky completed Notes from Underground and began work towards Crime and Punishment (1866). The major novels of his late period are The Idiot (1868), Demons (1871-2) and The Brothers Karamazov (1879-80). He died in 1881.<br /><br />(Russian: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16321565.____________________________" title="Фёдор Михайлович Достоевский" rel="nofollow noopener">Фёдор Михайлович Достоевский</a>) (see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17062424.Fiodor_Dosto_evski" title="Fiodor Dostoïevski" rel="nofollow noopener">Fiodor Dostoïevski</a>/<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18606930.Fi_dor_M__Dostoievski" title="Fiódor M. Dostoievski" rel="nofollow noopener">Fiódor M. Dostoievski</a>).</span>
  <a data-text-id="8440117379729393047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/4111509">Quotes from White Nights</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;But how could you live and have no story to tell?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/132639-but-how-could-you-live-and-have-no-story-to">1731 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I am a dreamer. I know so little of real life that I just can't help re-living such moments as these in my dreams, for such moments are something I have very rarely experienced. I am going to dream about you the whole night, the whole week, the whole year. I feel I know you so well that I couldn't have known you better if we'd been friends for twenty years. You won't fail me, will you? Only two minutes, and you've made me happy forever. Yes, happy. Who knows, perhaps you've reconciled me with myself, resolved all my doubts.<br /><br />When I woke up it seemed to me that some snatch of a tune I had known for a long time, I had heard somewhere before but had forgotten, a melody of great sweetness, was coming back to me now. It seemed to me that it had been trying to emerge from my soul all my life, and only now-<br /><br />If and when you fall in love, may you be happy with her. I don't need to wish her anything, for she'll be happy with you. May your sky always be clear, may your dear smile always be bright and happy, and may you be for ever blessed for that moment of bliss and happiness which you gave to another lonely and grateful heart. Isn't such a moment sufficient for the whole of one's life?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/188743-i-am-a-dreamer-i-know-so-little-of-real">1263 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/4111509">More quotes&hellip;</a>
